So it's more about what's easy or hard, so I do think that ultimately yeah, I think everything. Everything is automatable. Amazing, okay. So last year you coined the term "vibe coding" and today we're in a world that feels a little bit more serious, more "agentic engineering." What do you think is the difference between the two and what would you actually call what we're in today? Yeah, so I would say vibe coding is about raising the floor for everyone in terms of what they can do in software. So the floor rises, everyone can vibe code anything, and that's amazing, incredible. But then I would say agentic engineering is about preserving the quality bar of what existed before in professional software. You're not allowed to introduce vulnerabilities due to vibe coding. You are still responsible for your software just as before, but can you go faster? And spoiler is you can, but how do you do that properly? And so to me, agentic engineering, when I call it that, is because I do think it's kind of an engineering discipline. You have these agents which are these spiky entities, they're a bit fallible, a little bit stochastic, but they are extremely powerful, and it's how do you coordinate them to go faster without sacrificing your quality bar? And doing that well and correctly is the realm of agentic engineering. So I kind of see them as different, like one is about maybe raising the floor and the other is about, you know, extrapolating. And what I'm seeing I think is there is a very high ceiling on agentic engineer capability and you know people used to talk about the 10x engineer previously. I think that this is magnified a lot more. Like 10x is not the speed-up you gain and I think it does seem to me like people who are very good at this peak a lot more than 10x from my perspective right now. I really like that framing. One thing that when Sam Altman came to AI Ascent last year, one memorable thing he said was that people of different generations use ChatGPT differently. So if you're in your 30s you use it as a Google search replacement, but if you're in your teens ChatGPT is your gateway to the internet. What is the parallel here in coding today? If we were to watch two people code using Open-Claw, Claude Code, Codex, one you'd consider mediocre at it and one you would consider fully AI-native, how would you describe the difference? I mean I think it's a lot just trying to get the most out of the tools that are available, utilizing all of their features, investing into your own kind of setup. So just like previously, all the engineers are used to basically getting the most out of the tools you use, either it's Vim or VS Code or now it's, you know, Claude Code or Codex or so on. So just investing into your setup and utilizing a lot of the, you know, tools that are available to you, and I think it just kind of looks like that. I do think that maybe a related thought is a lot of people are maybe hiring for this, right, because they want to hire strong agentic engineers. I do think that what I'm seeing is that most people have still not refactored their hiring process for agentic engineer capability, right? If you're giving out puzzles to solve then this is still the old paradigm. I would say that hiring has to look like: "Give me a really big project and see someone implement that big project." Let's write say a Twitter clone for agents and then make it really good, make it really secure, and then have some agents simulate some activity on this Twitter, and then I'm going to use 10 Codex 5.4xh to try to break the website that you deployed and they should not be able to break it. And so maybe it looks like that, right? And so yeah, watching people in that setting and building bigger projects and utilizing the tooling is maybe what I would look at for the most part. And as agents do more, what human skill do you think becomes more valuable, not less? So yeah, it's a good question. I think well right now the answer is that the agents are kind of like these intern entities, right? So it's remarkable you basically still have to be in charge of the aesthetics, the judgment, the taste, and a little bit of oversight. Maybe one of my favorite examples of the weirdness of agents is for MenuGen. You sign up with a Google account but you purchase credits using a Stripe account, and both of them have email addresses. And my agent actually tried to basically... when you purchase credits, it assigned it using the email address from Stripe to the Google email address. Like there wasn't a persistent user ID for people, it was trying to match up the email addresses, but you could use a different email address for your Stripe and your Google and basically it would not associate the funds. And so this is the kind of thing that these agents still will make mistakes about: like why would you use email addresses to try to cross-correlate the funds? They can be arbitrary, you can use different emails, etc. Like this is such a weird thing to do. So I think people have to be in charge of this spec, this plan, and I actually don't even like the plan mode, I mean obviously it's very useful, but I think there's something more general here where you have to work with your agent to design a spec that is very detailed. And maybe it's basically the docs, and then get the agents to write them. And you're in charge of the oversight and the top-level categories, but the agents are doing a lot of the under the hood. And so I think you're not caring about some of the details. So as an example also with arrays or tensors in neural networks, there's a ton of details between PyTorch and NumPy and all the different like Pandas and so on for all the different little API details. And I already forgot about the keep-dims versus keep-dim, or whether it's dim or axis or reshape or permute or transpose. I don't remember this stuff anymore, right, because you don't have to. This is the kind of details that are handled by the intern because they have very good recall. But you still have to know, for example, that there's an underlying tensor, there's an underlying view and then you can manipulate the view of the same storage or you can have different storage which would be less efficient. And so you still have to have an understanding of what this stuff is doing and some of the fundamentals so that you're not copying memory around unnecessarily and so on. But the details of the APIs are now handed off. You're in charge of the taste, the engineering, the design and that it makes sense, and that you're asking for the right things. And that you're saying that okay these have to be unique user IDs that we're going to tie everything to. And so you're doing some of the design and development and the engineers are doing the fill in the blanks. And that's currently kind of where we are and I think that's what everyone of course is seeing, I think, right now. Do you think there's a chance that this taste and judgment matters less over time or will the ceiling just keep rising? Yeah, it's a good question. I would say I mean I'm hoping that it improves. I think probably the reason it doesn't improve right now is again it's not part of the RL. There's probably no aesthetics cost or reward or it's not good enough or something like that. I do think that when you actually look at the code, sometimes I get a little bit of a heart attack because it's not like super amazing code necessarily all the time. It's very bloated and there's a lot of copy-paste and there's awkward abstractions that are brittle and like it works but it's just really gross. And I do hope that this can improve in future models. A good example also is this micro-GPT project which... where I was trying to simplify LLM training to be as simple as possible. The models hate this. They can't do it. I tried, I kept trying to prompt an LLM to simplify more, simplify more, and it just can't. You feel like you're outside of the RL circuits. It feels like you're obviously pulling teeth. It's not like light speed. So I think I do think that people still remain in charge of this, but I do think that there's nothing fundamental again that's preventing it, it's just the labs haven't done it yet almost. Yeah. I'd love to come back to this idea of jagged forms of intelligence. You wrote a little bit about this with a very thought-provoking piece around animals versus ghosts. And the idea is that we're not building animals, we are summoning ghosts. And these are jagged forms of intelligence that are shaped by data and reward functions but not by intrinsic motivation or fun or curiosity or empowerment, things that kind of came about via evolution. Why does that framing matter and what does it actually change about how you build and deploy and evaluate or even trust them? Yeah, so I think the reason I wrote about this is because I'm trying to wrap my head around what these things are, right? Because if you have a good model of what they are or are not, then you're going to be more competent at using them. And I do think that I don't know if it has... I'm not sure if it actually has like real power. I think it's a little bit of philosophizing but I do think that I think it's just coming to terms with the fact that these things are not, you know, animal intelligences. Like if you yell at them, they're not going to work better or worse or it doesn't have any impact. And it's all just kind of like these statistical simulation circuits where the substrate is pre-training, so like statistics. And then but then there's RL bolted on top, so it kind of like increases these appendages. And maybe it's just kind of a mindset of what I'm coming into or what's likely to work or not likely to work or how to modify it. But I don't actually... I don't know that I have like, here are the five obvious outcomes of how to make your system better. It's more just being suspicious of it and figuring it out over time. That's where it starts. Okay, so you are so deep in working with agents that don't just chat. They have real permissions, they have local context, they actually take action on your behalf. What does the world look like when we all start to live in that world? Yeah, I think... I think a lot of people probably here are excited about what this agent-native agentic environment looks like and everything has to be rewritten. Everything is still fundamentally written for humans and has to be moved around. I still use most of the time when I use different frameworks or libraries or things like that, they still have docs that are fundamentally written for humans. This is my favorite pet peeve. Like I don't... why are people still telling me what to do? Like I don't want to do anything. What is the thing I should copy-paste to my agent? Like so it's just every time I'm told, you know, go to this URL or something like that, it's just like, "Ugh." You know. So everyone is I think excited about how do we decompose the workloads that need to happen into fundamentally sensors over the world, actuators over the world, how do we make it agent-native, basically describe it to agents first and then have a lot of automation around the... yeah around data structures that are very legible to the LLMs. So I think yeah, I'm hoping that there's a lot of agent-first infrastructure out there. And that you know for MenuGen famously when I wrote the... not I'm not sure how famously, but when I wrote the blog post about MenuGen a lot of the work or a lot of the trouble was not even writing the code for MenuGen, it was deploying it on Vercel. Because I had to work with all these different services and I had to string them up and I had to go to their settings in the menus and configure my DNS and it was just so annoying. And so that's a good example of I would hope that MenuGen... that I could give a prompt to an LLM, build MenuGen, and that I didn't have to touch anything and it's deployed in that same way on the internet. I think that would be a good kind of test for whether or not a lot of our infrastructure is becoming more and more agent-native. And then ultimately I would say yeah, I do think we're going towards a world where there's agent representation for people and for organizations. And you know I'll have my agent talk to your agent to figure out some of the details of our meetings or things like that. So I do think that that's roughly where things are going, but yeah, I think everyone in here is excited about that. I really like the visual analogy of sensors and actuators. I actually hadn't thought of that, that's super interesting. Okay, I think we have to end on a question about education because you are probably one of the very best in the world at making complex technical concepts simple and deeply thoughtful about how we design education around it. What still remains worth learning deeply when intelligence gets cheap as we move into the next era of AI? Yeah, there was a tweet that blew my mind recently and I keep thinking about it like every other day. It was something along the lines of, "You can outsource your thinking but you can't outsource your understanding." And so yeah, because I still... I'm still part of the system and I still have to somehow information still has to make it into my brain. And I feel like I'm becoming a bottleneck of just even knowing what are we trying to build, why is it worth doing, how do I direct... you know how do I direct my agents and so on. So I do still think that ultimately something has to direct the thinking and the processing and so on, and that's still kind of fundamentally constrained somehow by understanding. And this is one reason I also was very excited about all the LLM knowledge bases because I feel like that's a way for me to process information and anytime I see a different projection onto information, I always feel like I gain insight. So it's really just a lot of prompts for me to do synthetic data generation kind of over some fixed data. So I really enjoy... whenever I read an article, I have my wiki that's being built up from these articles and I love asking questions about things. And I think that ultimately these are tools to enhance understanding in a certain way, and this is still kind of a bit of a bottleneck because then you can't direct the... you can't be a good director if you still... because the LLMs certainly don't excel at understanding, you still are uniquely in charge of that. So yeah, I think tools to that effect I think are incredibly interesting and exciting. I'm excited to be back here in a couple years and to see if we've been fully automated out of the loop and they actually take care of understanding as well. Thank you so much for joining us, Andrej. We really appreciate it. Thank you.

How to Build the Future: Demis Hassabis

Y Combinator(2.2M) ·40:57 ·26.1K views · 2026-04-29
Demis Hassabis (CEO, Google DeepMind) Garry Tan (CEO, Y Combinator)
1.
TL;DR: AGI is likely achievable by 2030 through the refinement of current architectures and 1-2 remaining breakthroughs in memory and reasoning; the most immediate, defensible opportunities for founders lie in "Deep Tech" where AI intersects with physical sciences and complex...

TL;DR: AGI is likely achievable by 2030 through the refinement of current architectures and 1-2 remaining breakthroughs in memory and reasoning; the most immediate, defensible opportunities for founders lie in "Deep Tech" where AI intersects with physical sciences and complex search spaces.

The Path to AGI: Missing Components and Architecture

  • The Final Architecture: Hassabis believes current components—large-scale pre-training, RLHF, and chain-of-thought—will be part of the final AGI framework. However, the industry is 50/50 on whether current techniques just need scaling or if 1-2 major "big ideas" are still required.
  • Unsolved Challenges:
      • Continual Learning: Systems currently lack the ability to adapt gracefully to new knowledge without forgetting or requiring massive retraining.
      • Memory Efficiency: While context windows have reached millions of tokens, they currently function as "brute force" working memory. AGI requires smarter episodic memory and better indexing to reduce the cost of retrieving relevant information.
      • Consistent Reasoning: Despite solving IMO-level math, models still make elementary errors. Hassabis suggests a missing "introspection" layer is needed for models to monitor their own thought processes and avoid logic loops.
  • The Return of Reinforcement Learning (RL): Techniques from AlphaGo, such as Monte Carlo tree search and "thinking modes," are being integrated back into foundation models to move beyond simple pattern matching toward active planning.

Model Scaling and the Power of Distillation

  • The Frontier vs. The Edge: While frontier models (like Gemini Pro) define what is possible, Hassabis emphasizes that Google’s strength is packing that power into "Flash" and "Nano" models.
      • Performance Gap: Distilled models are reaching 90-95% of frontier capability at 1/10th the cost and significantly lower latency.
      • No Density Limit: There is currently no observable theoretical limit to how much "intelligence" can be distilled into smaller models.
  • Multi-modal Advantage: Gemini was built to be natively multimodal from the start. This provides a "world model" foundation essential for robotics and understanding intuitive physics, which Hassabis views as a key competitive advantage.
  • Local Processing: Powerful local models are necessary for privacy, security (personal data), and robotics (low-latency physical interaction), likely orchestrated by larger cloud models for high-level reasoning.

The Future of Agents and "1000x" Engineering

  • The Transition Period: We are moving from a "toy/demonstration" phase to a period where agents add fundamental value. Hassabis notes that while engineers can now do "1000x" the work of a 2000s-era engineer, we have yet to see a "AAA" hit (like a top-tier video game) created entirely by an agentic system.
  • Human "Soul" and Craft: AGI will need to move beyond "move 37" (creativity within a game) to "inventing the game itself." Until then, high-level taste and human "soul" remain the bottle-neck for creating meaningful products.
  • Autonomous Learning: Current agents fail because they cannot adapt to specific user contexts. Hassabis argues that cracking "continual learning" is the prerequisite for "fire and forget" autonomous agents.

AI for Science: The "Root-Node" Problems

  • The AlphaFold Success: Over 3 million researchers use AlphaFold, and Hassabis predicts almost every drug discovered from now on will involve the tool.
  • The "Alpha" Recipe for Success: Scientific domains are ripe for breakthroughs if they possess:

1. A massive combinatorial search space (too large for brute force).

2. A clear, quantifiable objective function.

3. A high-quality simulator or enough data to generate "synthetic" in-distribution data.

  • Future Breakthroughs:
      • Virtual Cell: DeepMind aims to build a full working simulation of a cell within 10 years, though it currently faces a hardware bottleneck in imaging live cells at nanometer resolution without destroying them.
      • Materials & Math: Hassabis identifies material science and mathematics (e.g., solving P vs NP or Millennium Prize problems) as the next major frontiers.

Strategic Advice for Founders

  • Intercept the Tech Curve: Founders must build for where AI will be in 2-3 years, not where it is today.
  • Target "Deep Tech": The most defensible startups combine AI with another deep technology (atoms, biology, materials). These interdisciplinary areas are "safe" from being disrupted by general foundation model updates because they require specialized expertise and physical-world integration.
  • The 2030 AGI Timeline: Hassabis advises founders to assume AGI will emerge by 2030. If your "Deep Tech" journey is a 10-year project, you must plan for how your company will leverage or integrate with AGI halfway through its development.

Notable Quotes

"Step one was solve intelligence—i.e., build AGI—and then step two was use it to solve everything else." — Demis Hassabis
"I often get the impression with our systems and our competitor systems that they're almost overthinking... it'll realize [a move] is a blunder but it can't find anything better so it kind of goes back to that move and does it anyway." — Demis Hassabis
"Nothing that's really long-lasting and worthwhile is easy... if you have belief and conviction in your idea, why it's different this time... there's huge value to be built there." — Demis Hassabis
"I sometimes call it my 'Einstein test': can you train a system with the knowledge cutoff of 1901 and will it come up with what Einstein did in 1905? Once that is possible, we're on the verge of these systems being able to invent something truly novel." — Demis Hassabis
Demis Hassabis has had one of the most unusual careers in tech. He was a chess prodigy as a kid, then designed his first hit video game, Theme Park, at 17. He then went back to school, got a PhD in cognitive neuroscience, and published foundational work on how memory and imagination work in the brain. In 2010, he co-founded DeepMind with one mission: solve intelligence. And I think they've done it. Since then, his lab has gone on to do things most people thought were decades away. AlphaGo beat a world champion at Go. AlphaFold cracked protein structure prediction, a 50-year grand challenge in biology, and they gave it away for free to every scientist on earth. That work won him the Nobel Prize in Chemistry last year. Today Demis leads Google DeepMind where he's building Gemini and pushing toward the same goal he set when he was a teenager: artificial general intelligence. Please welcome Demis Hassabis. So you've been thinking about AGI longer than almost anyone. So I think they've come such a long way now and we've proven out so many things about what they can do. I can't see a world in which we'll sort of realize in a couple of years this was a dead end. That doesn't make sense to me. But there still might be one or two things missing on top of of you know what we already know works. So continual learning, long-term reasoning, some aspects of memory—these are still unsolved. And how to get the systems to be more consistent across the board. Stripe Sessions 2026 | Developer keynote

Stripe(100.0K) ·36:45 ·1.1K views · 2026-04-29
Stripe is shifting its focus toward "Agent Experience" (AX), evolving its platform to make AI agents smarter, more capable, and safer through deterministic tools, human-in-the-loop guardrails, and agent-optimized documentation.
TL;DR Stripe is shifting its focus toward "Agent Experience" (AX), evolving its platform to make AI agents smarter, more capable, and safer through deterministic tools, human-in-the-loop guardrails, and agent-optimized documentation. Rapid Adoption of AI Agents: Michelle Bu...

TL;DR

Stripe is shifting its focus toward "Agent Experience" (AX), evolving its platform to make AI agents smarter, more capable, and safer through deterministic tools, human-in-the-loop guardrails, and agent-optimized documentation.

The Evolution of Developer Experience (DX) to Agent Experience (AX)

  • Rapid Adoption of AI Agents: Michelle Bu (Lead, API Design and DX) reported that 91% of Stripe engineers now use AI tools, with internal "Minion" agents shipping over 1,000 PRs per week.
  • Documentation Traffic Shift: Agent traffic to Stripe’s documentation increased 10x in a single year, growing from 5% to nearly 40% of total traffic.
  • The Problem with Generic Agents: LLMs often rely on outdated training data, leading to the use of deprecated APIs (e.g., the Plans API) or overly complex integration patterns (e.g., Card Elements vs. modern Checkout).

Making Agents Smarter with Context and Benchmarks

  • Stripe Integration Benchmark: A new tool to measure an agent’s ability to complete Stripe tasks in a controlled, realistic environment to ensure accuracy and best practices.
  • Agent-Optimized Documentation:
      • Raw Markdown Access: Developers and agents can now append `.md` to any Stripe documentation URL to receive clean, lightweight content without headers, footers, or UI noise.
      • Stripe Blueprints: A new "Copy for LLM" button provides structured instructions, required objects, and API sequences specifically for agent consumption.
  • Agent Skills: These load Stripe’s specialized expertise directly into an agent's context window, guiding them toward the most modern integration shapes.

Increasing Agent Capability through Deterministic Tools

  • Model Context Protocol (MCP): Stripe’s MCP server allows agents to call a curated set of API endpoints and use tools like the "Stripe Integration Recommender" to ask clarifying questions about business logic.
  • Stripe CLI & Sandboxing:
      • Agents can now use the Stripe CLI to provision isolated sandboxes without requiring manual API credentials from the user.
      • Sandboxes provide real infrastructure for agents to test code end-to-end before a human claims the account.
  • Stripe Checkout Studio: A visual builder that allows for rapid prototyping; it can scan GitHub codebases and automatically generate PRs for UI changes like adding phone number collection or promotional consent.

Ensuring Trust with Guardrails and Custom Logic

  • Agent-Tagged API Keys: Users can now issue keys specifically for agents, providing full observability and the ability to treat agents as distinct actors with unique permissions.
  • Programmable Approval Rules:
      • Human-in-the-Loop: Safety-by-default rules can block agent actions (e.g., refunds over $100) and trigger a request for human review.
      • Structured Errors: If an action is blocked, the API returns a structured error that the agent can interpret to automatically submit an approval request.
  • Stripe Workflows & Custom Objects:
      • Custom Actions: Workflows can now trigger external actions, such as notifying a sales team in Slack when a refund occurs.
      • Business-Specific Logic: Users can define "Custom Objects" (e.g., a "Customer Follow-up" record) within Stripe to track processes that exist outside standard payment flows.
"In 2025, agent traffic to Stripe’s documentation 10xed in a single year... on this trajectory, agents will soon be reading more documentation than humans." — Michelle Bu
"We’re empowering agents—this little blobby non-deterministic thing—in three ways: making them smarter, making them more capable, and making them trustworthy." — Michelle Bu
"Writing code is just one part of running a business on Stripe... you want agents to not only help build your business, but also operate it." — Michelle Bu
"We’ve been obsessing over developer experience for 15 years. Agent experience is just the next chapter." — Michelle Bu

Our agent can read the contents of Slack, it’s optimized for searching our humongous codebase and it’ll find the right code to change based on the context. It looks like it cooked for a second, it analyzed the conversation to determine the repository and workspace, and now it is off. We’ll check back on this later. The agent you just saw is called a Minion. And Minions now ship over 1,000 PRs to production every week. In all these cases, humans just have to review and approve. But beyond these one-shot PRs, in the month of March over 57,000 PRs were created with the help of AI coding assistants, which is a 140% increase in just three months. And this change isn’t only internal to Stripe. In 2025, agent traffic to Stripe’s documentation 10xed in a single year. We went from agents representing less than 5% of our docs traffic to nearly 40%. And on this trajectory, agents will soon be reading more documentation than humans. These data points validate that we’re experiencing a step function change in how development works. But despite all this innovation, there are still basics that we need to help our new agent friends with. Let me show you an example of an attempt at a Stripe integration from just a few weeks ago. Here I’m trying to build a side business that takes upfront payments and start long-term engagements, but I also want to collect recurring subscription fees. This business is called Vibe Check. I’m going to use Claude Code to add payments to my site. Here I’ve written a prompt describing my business model and asking it to set up invoices and subscriptions. Claude correctly recommends Stripe for payments, of course. But watch what happens as we actually dig into the agent’s suggestions. Coding agents have improved rapidly, but they still make some common errors because they’re often relying on outdated training data. And they don’t know what they don’t know. So, when setting up subscriptions, the model suggests using the Plans API. That might sound reasonable, but I know that API was deprecated in 2019, because I was at Stripe then. The agent also didn’t ask me any clarifying questions about my customer base or the way that my business operates, which is actually really, really important for selecting the right Stripe integration shape. Next, it asks me to plug in API keys to continue, but I haven’t signed up for Stripe yet. I don’t want to break this development flow, so I’ll just ask the agent to keep going and get my API keys later. Claude starts writing code, and if we look at the code, we can see that it’s getting the user’s payment details with a Card Element. Many of my users are in the EU, where credit cards are not the only payment method. Card Elements are focused on collecting card details and so they don’t support preferred local payment methods out of the box. That means my integration now has to manage payment method specific UI and error handling, which adds a lot more complexity and vibe coded code for me to maintain. And because I haven’t provided an API key yet, Card Element hasn’t actually been injected into the site, so it just looks like an empty rectangle. The code looks plausible and like it could work, but we can’t actually test it end to end. So now I need to stop what I was doing entirely and go through Stripe’s sign-up flow. Granted, that was still much faster than reading the docs and writing the integration myself. But without my prior Stripe knowledge, we would have ended up with a lot of problems that would have bitten us later. The developer experience that agent was relying on was built for humans. But now, for both Stripe and for the broader industry, agent experience is becoming a large part of the developer experience. And that’s why we’re empowering agents, this little blobby non-deterministic thing, in three ways. First, we’re making agents smarter by giving them context on Stripe’s best practices. Second, we’re making agents more capable with deterministic tools for building and interacting with Stripe’s products. And finally, we’re making agents trustworthy with guardrails that prevent them from going rogue. So let’s take a closer look at each of these. First, making them smarter. When agents aren’t guided, they default to what they’ve been trained on. And that’s not necessarily what’s right or what’s best for your business. For example, last year we released Elements with Checkout Sessions, which is a dramatically simpler way to build checkout that handles tax, pricing, and payments orchestration right out of the box. But because of outdated training data, probably some open source projects, some old Stripe docs, maybe a five-year-old Reddit thread that recommends the Card Element, our data now shows that agents consistently default to recommending Payment Intents. This is recommending an unnecessarily complex integration architecture, even though we now have more modern solutions. So, this means if you’re a customer, you’re starting off your Stripe journey on the wrong foundation. And when you consider this at a macro level, including the increased speed at which agents are able to write code and build, this outdated recommendation gets replicated across thousands of repos very quickly. The scale of this mistake has a much wider impact than it did just a couple years ago. So to monitor agent performance and correctness, we created a Stripe integration benchmark. These benchmarks measure an agent’s ability to complete common Stripe tasks in a realistic environment. And because the benchmark establishes a controlled baseline, we can experiment with how to improve the agent’s performance. We’ve used the insights in our experimentation to make a number of enhancements, starting with our documentation. Our docs were designed to be easily scannable by the human eye. But now, they also have to be as lightweight as possible to fit into precious context space and to minimize token waste for all of you. So to address this constraint, we’ve made all of our documentation available as raw markdown. By appending .md to any URL, an agent gets all the content without headers, footers, and UI elements. Stripe’s Agent Skills also load our expertise right into the agent’s context window, which guides it to the right choices before it makes a single line of code. Another enhancement is to Stripe Blueprints. Blueprints, like docs, were originally built for humans as a step-by-step way to build a Stripe integration. However, an agent doesn’t use a mouse and so they don’t need a visual clicky guide. We’ve added a Copy for LLM button to our human blueprints, which copies a structured set of instructions. These instructions define the required objects, the recommended product settings, and the exact sequence of API calls to make and any object state changes to monitor for, all so your agent can build you a great integration. Because of our benchmarking and product enhancements, we’ve improved the context that the agents have at the tips of their blobby hands. Your experience with agents is now a lot smarter. Did you see his little glasses? But knowing the right answer isn’t the same as being able to act on it. Today, most platforms don’t actually give agents a way to do things. Your smarter agent can tell you what to do, but it can’t do it for you. We’re changing that and we’re making agents not just smarter, but also more capable. Humanoid Robots and the Gap Between Hype and Reality | Bloomberg Primer

Bloomberg Originals(5.0M) ·24:02 ·65.2K views · 2026-04-29
The humanoid robot industry is transitioning from viral hype to "Physical AI" commercialization, but the sector faces a massive "robot data gap" and significant engineering hurdles before achieving trillion-dollar market projections.
TL;DR The humanoid robot industry is transitioning from viral hype to "Physical AI" commercialization, but the sector faces a massive "robot data gap" and significant engineering hurdles before achieving trillion-dollar market projections. Defining Physical AI: Unlike Large...

TL;DR

The humanoid robot industry is transitioning from viral hype to "Physical AI" commercialization, but the sector faces a massive "robot data gap" and significant engineering hurdles before achieving trillion-dollar market projections.

The Shift to Physical AI and the "Data Gap"

  • Defining Physical AI: Unlike Large Language Models (LLMs) that are "book smart," physical AI integrates sensors, batteries, and actuators to allow robots to navigate and manipulate the real world.
      • Bernt Børnich (CEO, 1X): Notes that the behavior of their robot, NEO, is governed by a neural network that translates images and tactile forces into motor commands.
  • The Robot Data Gap: AI models like ChatGPT were trained on trillions of words from the internet, but there is no equivalent massive dataset for physical robot motions.
      • Data Collection Methods: Companies are bridging the gap through simulation (virtual training at scale), video harvesting (learning from YouTube), and teleoperation (humans remotely "puppeteering" robots to record data).
      • The "Flywheel" Effect: Professor Ken Goldberg (UC Berkeley) describes a cycle where robots collect data on the job, which is then filtered and used to train superior subsequent models.

Investment Frenzy and Market Projections

  • High-Stakes Betting: Billions of dollars were invested in 2025 alone, driven by a race between tech giants like OpenAI, Google, Tesla, and Nvidia.
      • Nvidia’s Role: Beyond providing GPUs for data centers, Nvidia is positioning its chips and software to serve as the "brains" inside the robots.
  • Hyperbolic Projections: Elon Musk has claimed that Tesla’s "Optimus" robot could eventually generate $30 trillion in annual revenue, though many experts view these figures as "far-fetched" compared to the current technical reality.
  • Paradigm Shift: The industry is moving toward a multi-trillion dollar market estimate by 2050, shifting the focus from "party tricks" to functional labor in factories and homes.

China’s Strategic Manufacturing Advantage

  • Government Support: Under the "Made in China 2025" plan, the Chinese government has designated robotics a strategic priority, pledging roughly $140 billion (one trillion yuan) to promote emerging technologies.
  • Vertical Integration: China dominates the humanoid market (leading in shipments as of 2025) due to its highly integrated supply chain.
      • Scale and Speed: Chinese firms like AgiBot, UBTech, and Unitree benefit from domestic access to sensors, AI chips, and batteries, allowing them to manufacture more cheaply and iterate faster than global competitors.
  • Economic Drivers: China is racing to deploy industrial robots to offset a rapidly shrinking workforce.

Real-World Implementation and Bottlenecks

  • Industrial Pilot Programs: Companies including BMW, Amazon, and GXO are currently testing humanoids in controlled warehouse environments.
      • Niche Success: GXO uses humanoids for tasks in extreme environments, such as freezers, to prevent human discomfort and injury.
  • Critical Performance Gaps: Current models still struggle with basic human traits:
      • Dexterity and Reliability: Robots frequently drop or damage products and require human "audits" to ensure accuracy.
      • Engineering Limits: Slow movement speeds and limited battery life remain significant barriers to achieving a positive Return on Investment (ROI).
  • Workforce Impact: While there is a projected gap of two million unfilled manufacturing jobs in the US by 2033, experts suggest humanoids will remain "co-workers" rather than total replacements for the foreseeable future due to the complexity of human tasks.
"In ChatGPT you're looking at a string of words and then you're trying to generate what would be the next appropriate string of words... In a robot what we need to see is a string of images... and then we need to see a string of motions." — Professor Ken Goldberg, UC Berkeley
"Everything is just data. We are seeing a very clear correlation between the amount of data and how many different tasks we can do and how successful we are at these tasks." — Bernt Børnich, CEO of 1X
"The humanoids so far are not perfect. They damage product, they drop product, they may put product back in the wrong location... It's not really understood yet what the return on investment is going to be." — GXO Logistics Representative
This hand, this knee, and this head, are all small feats of engineering, decades in the making. And together, they're about to take a massive leap forward. Oh dear. Humanoid robots have exploded out of the lab and into the real world of late. Not always on the best footing. But I'm not just here to show you funny robot videos for the next half hour. Even though I could. Easily. Because behind silly hats and dancing is a very serious, very expensive, and very unproven bet: that AI-powered robots could one day transform labor as we know it. The interest in humanoid robotics has been around for a long, long time. What's changed, of course, is now we see that it's viable to commercialize this and start really applying some good business models. AI clearly plays a large part in this. The hope is in cracking the code of physical AI. Taking specialized artificial intelligence and putting it in a body with sensors, batteries, and actuators. These are very expensive and technologically complex actuators. It's a paradigm shift that could set humanoids up to become a multi-trillion dollar market by 2050. But for these massive figures to be realized, it won't be about party tricks or dance routines. It'll be about work. In homes, in hospitals, in factories, especially factories. When you really work with robots, you realize how challenging it is. There is a lot of what I sometimes call humanoid hype out there. Like a lot of hype. Coming from the most influential figures in the tech industry. But hype doesn't build a supply chain, or rewrite the global labor market. To do that you need—well, let's get into it, shall we? Bloomberg Primer: The Humanoid Condition. This robot runs on AI. Well, sometimes. For this demonstration, filmed in 2025, NEO needed some help from real humans and was operated remotely by a man named Turing. No, not that Turing. But don't worry, we'll get to that later. Looking beautiful, NEO. Did you hit your head? You okay? This particular humanoid is made by a company called 1X, a robotics startup founded by this actual human, Bernt Børnich. So this is NEO, this is the gamma version. This is what we've been doing the in-home tests among employees with. This is the one that's doing my laundry. And this is the last, let's call it like the last engineering version. The version that we're working on right now that's about to go into production is the unit that will go into the first customer homes. Essentially the entire behavior of NEO is AI, in the sense that it takes images, forces, tactile, and in general all the information that it has about the world in. There's a big neural network and out comes the actual motor commands all the way down to the forces and torques that it's exerting to be able to do everything from balance and walk to doing manipulation. Unlike a traditional robot that's programmed to do a specific task the same way every time, a robot that uses AI should, in theory, learn to do things over time in unpredictable environments. It's sometimes called physical AI. This is a category that, besides humanoids, includes other general intelligence robots and driverless cars. Basically, it's anything that uses motors, sensors, cameras, and AI models to make sense of the world around them. We are seeing a very clear correlation between the amount of data and how many different tasks we can do and how successful we are at these tasks. And that is really the beauty of how AI has changed robotics because there is no longer this limit where you need to write some specific code for every task. Everything is just data. 1X is just one of many humanoid robot companies that have burst onto the tech scene in recent years. The frenzy comes down to two little letters, which come with big checks. AI. The past few years we've seen explosive interest in development of and investment in AI. And a lot of people are seizing on this moment as a way to work on all kinds of related things including robots. It's a packed scene with billions of dollars in investments in 2025 alone and companies globally racking up huge valuations. People are betting big on humanoid robots because very high-profile people involved in the world of humanoid robotics are talking it up. There are so many humanoid robots here, and the AI that they need are physical AIs. Chipmakers like Nvidia are excited for their chips to power the brains of these humanoids. It needs a brain and Nvidia has a chip for that that can go into the robot itself. Then they have the software. Nvidia has largely a monopoly for AI GPUs that go into data centers for training large language and other AI models. So they have a stronghold there because just like generative AI tools, humanoid robotics models, real-world models, are also trained using the same technology. Other AI and tech giants like OpenAI, Google, and Tesla are also betting big that there's a lot of money to be made by taking AI off a screen and bringing it into the real world. And then, you have Elon Musk, who talks endlessly about humanoid robots. I think everyone on earth is going to have one, going to want one. His projections are even more, let's say far-fetched. Elon Musk has said that Optimus, Tesla's own humanoid robot, will be a product that can bring in 30 trillion dollars of revenue annually. And with numbers like that, there's hype. You increasingly see them showing up in ads, you see them at tech conferences, and there are lots and lots of videos of them. Sometimes you'll see them operating in person but they might be like tethered to something. So I feel like you're both seeing progress and you're also seeing this sense of it's not quite there yet. This is my students in our lab here, we call it the Auto Lab, for Automation Lab. And so students are working on various projects and we have a number of robots in here. Two-armed robot, we call it bi-manual. Professor Ken Goldberg at UC Berkeley has been interested in what makes robots work for a long time. In the last few years there's been a lot of excitement about the potential of artificial intelligence, specifically with the breakthrough of ChatGPT, which is a huge paradigm shift in the field of AI. And so that's the speculation, what is the GPT moment for robotics? But here's the thing: Large Language Models or LLMs, like the kind behind ChatGPT, are more book smart than a robot smart. In ChatGPT you're looking at a string of words and then you're trying to generate what would be the next appropriate string of words that goes with that. ChatGPT and other Large Language Models run on, well, mostly language. Think of it like having access to a massive library of trillions of words, photos, and videos from the internet. But a robot doesn't just generate words, it has to create motion. In a robot what we need to see is a string of images, a video, and then we need to see a string of motions, commands, that go to the robot arms to get it to do the right thing. To do that it needs data to learn from. A lot of data. When it comes to humanoid robots, data is so, so important. Without data, a system that relies on AI doesn’t know anything. If you want a robot to do something like folding laundry, for instance, you're going to have to have a ton of information about how laundry is folded so that it can learn and see patterns in that data. How do you pick up a piece of fabric? Do you use articulated fingers, which are extremely difficult to build? And how do you also hold onto things without squishing them? Robots learn from experience. And here's the problem, almost none of that physical experience has been recorded. No one has captured the motions of robots that go with the inputs that were generated coming from a camera. So we have a gap, what I call the robot data gap. That gap is a challenge and an opportunity, especially for the ones betting on physical AI. More data means better AI. The need for data for robots is so big companies are rushing to create libraries made of both real and artificial synthetic data. So people today are trying to collect this data to be able to train robots. The first one is simulation, to be able to simulate environments and robots moving in them. Think of it like creating a hyper-realistic environment the robot can learn in. Its superpower is scale. Thousands of robots in thousands of worlds, all learning at the same time. The problem is, virtual worlds and virtual robots can lack real-world detail. Another way to train humanoids is harvesting existing video of the real world. We do have a huge amount of video on YouTube and other places that show people manipulating things. But it's very difficult to get the full three-dimensional structure of a scene from a two-dimensional video. There is one option for delivering real human interactions in real-world conditions: Teleoperation. Where VR glasses and stylish outfits can turn these robots into a very expensive puppet. So there's someone behind the scenes, behind the curtain, who is controlling two arms, moving them around, and as that happens, the video images and the motions of the robot are recorded, and that provides data. But that's very, very slow. But there are farms, people out there doing that as we speak. Teleoperation has been criticized for its use in demonstrations to drum up excitement and money. But it is also a powerful training tool. Beyond teleoperation, there's another option: to let the robots just learn on the job. Goldberg calls it the flywheel. As it's working it's collecting data from its images and its motions. Then use that data by filtering it to train a larger model. And eventually that new model becomes better than the original model. Nailing an AI model and a training dataset that works is paramount for any robotics company hoping to make it in the humanoid biz. NEO is a robot for the home. We want every part of NEO to be inviting and made of materials that you would want to have around your family or in your living room. We’re using soft cover and this fabric for the face covering. When we visited 1X in 2025, they relied on a combination of tools to train their humanoids, including teleoperation and human input. Hi Turing. Since then, 1X says it increasingly uses something called a world model to train and run their humanoids. And it’s pulling from many of those data gap filling tactics. The video here on the left is generated by the model. It’s almost like a future vision of what the robot should do. And on the right is the robot completing the action. Similar to other AI video creation tools, the world model pulls from a vast library of images and video to generate a visualization of an action. Each time it generates the prediction of an action, it can learn from the resulting real-world action. In theory, this can help improve those predictions with every generation. Kind of like a flywheel, remember? Of course the actual goal of all these models and data is to get robots out of the lab and into the real world where they can continue to improve. And when it comes to doing that, there's one place that's been faster than the rest: Beijing, China. This is the first World Humanoid Robot Games, taking place in an old Olympic ice skating rink in Beijing. Look at all these humanoids. Running, jumping, dancing. Competitors here are gathered from all over the world to put humanoids to the mechanical test. I think the games are really interesting. There's tons of really cool events that you don't really see normally. It's very different, very unique, very special. When I first met our robot, when I first interacted with it, it was a little scary because you know you have this very heavy piece of machinery just running around and walking around next to you. Then you try to like keep your distance and stay away from it. But as I've gone to know it and work with it, I've developed this sort of connection with it. These games, which involve everything from boxing to football matches to—yes, work—are part of a large push in China to dominate the humanoid and AI robotics industry. China's unique interest in robots has to do with the government for the most part. The fact that they see it as a strategic priority. Back in 2015, the Made in China 2025 plan had mentioned robots along with EVs and semiconductors as future growth drivers. So a lot of money has been poured into this area and it continues to be so. In 2025, China pledged one trillion yuan, about 140 billion US dollars, to promote the advancement of emerging technologies. A big part of that being robotics. That kind of support has helped produce 140 Chinese companies all making humanoids. A number that has continued to grow so much that officials have warned of a potential bubble. Still, China is currently dominating the humanoid robotics market much like they've done with EVs. Companies like AgiBot, UBTech, and Unitree, among others, shipped the most humanoids out of any country in 2025. That's down in part to some serious chops when it comes to manufacturing cheaply and quickly. Which is a good thing if you're trying to get robots out in the world, fast. So much of the electronics industry has its supply chain from the sort of circuit level throughout China. And they have scale. Scale means better economics, and it means the ability to move quickly. It's really vertically integrated just like with EVs. They have a lot of companies that make components for these humanoid robots, whether it's sensors, AI chips, or batteries. The fact that they're able to source a supply of the components for these robots provides a clear advantage. That advantage in scale may come in handy. China already leads the world in the deployment of industrial robots as it races against a shrinking workforce. But for engineering, training, and investment to pay off, they're going to have to do something more than backflips. They're going to need to get a job. Sorry robots. I am an economist and I am studying the well-being implications of technological change. Technological anxiety and fear that technologies are going to take over our jobs is nothing new. What we need to think about before deploying humanoid robots at scale would be worker involvement in the process. So how these robots will be deployed would have huge implications for the quality of work that people will have. Robots aren't new. They've been in factories for decades. They're very good at performing routine tasks and physical tasks. Tasks like welding, painting, and assembling parts. But they are not multipurpose. And that's where the humanoids come in. Basically, a humanoid robot is designed to do the tasks that humans can do. It's estimated that almost two million manufacturing jobs in the US alone might go unfilled by 2033. But if humanoids are going to be part of closing that gap, they need to actually, well, move and problem-solve like a human, safely alongside people. And right now that’s only happening in a limited number of very controlled pilot tests run by some very big companies. For example, BMW is currently running a pilot and is reporting early data. Other big names testing these robots include Amazon and Hyundai. These trials are crucial when it comes to figuring out where humanoids work and where they don’t. They also provide, yes, data to fuel development while things like technology and regulation catch up. We started with pilots in humanoids about a year ago. My company is one of many testing out third-party humanoid robots in their warehouses. We have been working with robots for the last 10 years and we are at the forefront of piloting humanoids in our warehouse operations. It really is fascinating to see the new technology take foot in production in the warehouse environment. GXO has found some cases that humanoids might be useful for, like say, working in a freezer. We're able to deploy the humanoids in that environment where they can really work for an entire battery life, completing tasks and being directed by somebody who is outside of the freezer. This allows us to get work completed without putting our teammates in an uncomfortable situation working in that cold environment for a long time. But there's still a lot they're not great at. The humanoids so far are not perfect. They damage product, they drop product, and they may put product back in the wrong location. It is so important that our teammates are working alongside of them to do that. There is corrective action and audits done to make sure that the activity done by the humanoid is done accurately. Humanoids still face a number of engineering challenges that can create serious bottlenecks in a warehouse. They're slow, for one, they have limited battery life, and picking up a box or handling an object, things that we take for granted, require dexterity. It's something humanoids, which move using motors and actuators, have a hard time replicating. We do not see humanoids in the next couple years taking away many other tasks that our teammates perform. The tasks are too complex and too important from a quality perspective to go entirely to humanoids. And it's not really understood yet what the return on investment is going to be based on the finalized technologies that are coming out. In manufacturing you have a very fixed production process. You can shield off human beings and you can let the humanoid robot perform. While in a hospital setting, it's a semi-structured environment. There are protocols in how processes should be done and how patients should be cared for, but still there are so many uncertainties that can arise. In fact, it's about life and death type of situations and the costs of making a mistake are very, very high. So will humanoids one day overcome their technical challenges, recoup investments, and become indispensable companions? Folding our laundry, watering our plants, and building our cars? Or just be a novelty giving way to robots of other shapes and sizes? I've been in this field for 40 years and it is fascinating to me how much it has had a resurgence again and again over the years. I’m thrilled about that. I'm excited because I feel that this field's got many years of questions and research that need to be done. So in some sense I don't want to sound discouraging. I'm very actually optimistic about the future, but I think it's important again to just be realistic about the timeframe. In the meantime, I'll leave you with this: A humanoid robot, waving to you from a balcony, as a drone pulls slowly and dramatically away. That feels like a decent way to end. For now.

Live podcast with Jamie Dimon - CEO of JPMorgan Chase | Podcast | In Good Company

Jamie Dimon emphasizes that long-term corporate success is built on a "relentless" culture of grit, small autonomous teams, and high-integrity leadership, while warning that geopolitical fragmentation and slipping credit standards currently pose the greatest risks to global stability.
TL;DR Jamie Dimon emphasizes that long-term corporate success is built on a "relentless" culture of grit, small autonomous teams, and high-integrity leadership, while warning that geopolitical fragmentation and slipping credit standards currently pose the greatest risks to...

TL;DR

Jamie Dimon emphasizes that long-term corporate success is built on a "relentless" culture of grit, small autonomous teams, and high-integrity leadership, while warning that geopolitical fragmentation and slipping credit standards currently pose the greatest risks to global stability.

Leadership and Corporate Culture

  • Driving Culture Through Grit: Dimon argues that culture is not about words on a page but about "driving it with grit and courage" in every meeting, hire, and firing.
      • Success stems from a "serve-the-client" scheme rather than a compensation-based scheme.
      • The "Neural Network": JPMorgan functions as a global web where employees can call anyone across the firm for immediate answers, a structure Dimon believes AI cannot replace.
  • Combating Bureaucracy: Bureaucracy, complacency, and arrogance are the primary threats to large organizations.
      • "Rope-a-Dope" Meetings: Dimon cancels meetings where information isn't shared beforehand or where no clear decisions are made.
      • King for a Day: To force action, Dimon asks employees, "If you were king for a day, what would you do?" This prevents people from simply "admiring problems."
      • Eliminating "Jerks": A winning culture requires removing high-performers who are "all about themselves" and ruin team dynamics.
  • Governance and Board Dynamics: To ensure honest feedback, the JPMorgan board meets without Dimon at every meeting.
      • Board presentations are mostly verbal and fast-paced to encourage engagement rather than scripted "preambles."

Risk Management and the Global Economy

  • Concerns Over Private Credit: While not yet a systemic threat at $1.7 trillion, Dimon warns of a "deterioration in underwriting standards" across the industry.
      • He predicts that the next credit recession will be "worse than people think" due to aggressive assumptions and weaker covenants.
  • Geopolitical Fragmentation: Dimon identifies geopolitics—specifically the wars in Ukraine and Iran and the US-China relationship—as the most significant threat to the "free and democratic world."
      • He warns against the fragmentation of Western alliances, noting that "Economic relationship is not just tariffs; it’s investment and regulatory rules."
  • Inflationary Pressures: Dimon remains skeptical that inflation is fully under control, citing global deficits, remilitarization, and green infrastructure needs as persistent inflationary drivers.
      • Stagflation Risk: He refuses to take the "worst-case" scenario of stagflation off the table, suggesting probabilities of higher interest rates are higher than the market expects.

Regional Competitiveness: The European Challenge

  • The "Slow Walk" to Decline: Dimon points out that 25 years ago, the US and European GDPs were equal; today, Europe is only 70% of the US level.
      • The Draghi Report: Dimon urges European leaders to adopt the 300 recommendations in the Draghi Report, specifically the Capital Markets Union and common bankruptcy laws.
  • Scale and Scope: Without a finished common market, European companies will struggle to compete with American and Chinese "juggernauts."
  • Friend-Sourcing: He advocates for "one big, beautiful free trade bill" between the US and Europe to strengthen economic and military alliances.

Technology and the Future of Work

  • AI Integration: JPMorgan has thousands of people deployed on AI use cases ranging from fraud detection to note-taking and hedging.
      • The Cyber Threat: Dimon views cyberattacks as the top technical risk, especially as "bad guys" use AI to find vulnerabilities.
  • Workforce Redeployment: Dimon acknowledges that AI may displace jobs faster than previous technological shifts. He urges governments and companies to focus on "income assistance" and "skills training" rather than just firing workers.
  • The 3.5-Day Work Week: Increased productivity from capital formation and AI will eventually lead to a shorter work week (3.5 days) and higher GDP per person.
      • Return to Office: Despite the long-term trend toward shorter weeks, Dimon remains a firm believer in in-office work for collaboration and socialization.

Notable Quotes

"Bureaucracy, complacency, and arrogance will take down a company. Bureaucracy is like the Petri dish of politics." — Jamie Dimon
"The most significant thing for the future of the free and democratic world is the geopolitics... The fragmentation of that would be the reason a book is written one day, 'How the West Was Lost.'" — Jamie Dimon
"I always ask: 'If you’re king for a day, what are you going to do?' Because people walk in and they admire problems. If you ask them what they’re going to do, for a lot of people, it’s almost crippling to try to answer." — Jamie Dimon
"We're not here to satisfy my employees that they don't want to [come to the office]. We're here for the client." — Jamie Dimon
Well first, I have to say a big thank you to all the speakers today. We have actually had ten of them on the podcast, so you can binge it this weekend. Now, one person we've been trying to get since we started the podcast four years ago is Jamie Dimon. Now, I have a philosophy in life that "no" is a rest stop on the way to "yes," and in this case, it has worked. But just Jamie, before we welcome you onto the stage, just a few snippets here. JPMorgan's origins trace back to 1799, to a company founded to supply water to a growing New York City. It quickly evolved into a bank and shaped its lasting legacy. Over the next two centuries, it grew alongside the economy, financing railroads, supporting industry, and expanding with global trade. But its role became most visible in moments of crisis. In 1907, as panic spread through the financial system, JPMorgan helped organize a response, stabilizing markets at a critical moment and exposing the need for a central bank. Through the Great Depression, it endured one of the most severe economic downturns in modern history. In 2000, the merger of JPMorgan and Chase Manhattan created a new kind of institution. And in 2008, it stepped into the center of the crisis, acquiring Bear Stearns and Washington Mutual. Built on generations of decisions and culture, today JPMorgan Chase stands among the largest and most influential financial institutions in the world. Well, warm welcome. Warm welcome. Well first, when it comes to risk you have to be really disciplined and examine it over and over and over with risk reports shared because things change and before you know it you're in trouble.
